Canada faoas a growing crisis in education* Gonditioas of toaoher- 

supply have deteriorated steadily during the past ten ^ears« though five 

years ago a group of oosipetent Alberta educators were of the opinion that 

by 1948 there would be a surplus of teacherss 

Alberta should oapitalise on the post-^r surplus of teachers 
whioh promises to develop in order to expand present educational 
services and to return teachers, especially those with inferior 
cualifloations, to hi^ schools, universities, and professional 
schools ^ere they may repair, ini|>rov 0 , or augsffint their academic 
and professional qualifications.^- 

A post-war surplus of teachers has failed to materialise. Public 
apathy toward education apparently continued throughout the war, and only 
now are there signs of any degree of concern for the plight of education* 
These are evidenced by articles in recent issues of popular nmgasines 
and in reports of such organisations as the Canadian Chamber of Gonn^roe* 

Adequately trained and competent teachers cannot be secured nor 

retained by HK)ralising on high ideals of service. Something more is 

needed than the presentation of the **greatmss of teaching** as drawn by 

one writer, no matter how true we may believe his words to bet 

To envision the better world that oould be* to 3aiow that it can be 
made only by building better personalities; to believe that it can 
be made in that wayi • • * to feel oneself part of a profession 
unselfishly dedloated to the finding and Imparting of vital and 
necessary truth-these faiths give meaning to life* Ho individual 
vdio has seen and felt and understood these things will be daimged 
by the strains of teaching, nor will he leave teaching for any other 

work*2 

Some time ago the orisls in education had developed to such a point 
that hundreds of teachers had gone into other occupations* !Tha influx

Tho effeets of the shortage of teaohers in Oaaada have been felt 
most severely la the followiijig areas^^ 

1# I’rofessional standards have been lowered shortening the period of 
professional training, b^ lowering entrance requirements of teacher 
training Institution®, and bj’ peruiltting unqtaalified persons to teaoh# 

Tho ratio of unqmllfied persons to room whioh should be In operation 
is not lease than 1 In 10# 

Zm One-room rural sohools have suffered more severely than any other 
type of sohool# One out of fifty of these schools is olosed, and one- 
half their tesohers are not fully qualified* 

3* Men have left the profession in such numbers that the ratio of men 
to women has dropped from about three men to seven women to almost foiar 
men to thirteen women# 

fhe purpose of this thesis is to explore the field of the sooial 
and eeonomio status of teachers with a view to presenting data which may 
be used to establish a relationship between suoh status and the present 
eduoatlonal crisis, if suoh a relationship exists*

Since the preparation of this thesis was part of a gronp stu^s? 
oovaring a much broader field than that of the thesis problem oertain 
departures from ordinary' study procedures were neeessery* Freparatory 
discussions were held under the direction of Bean La^erte to detertnii^ 
first, the scope of the Survey and second, the thesis problems that 
might be established within that scope# these were dsoided upon 

menders of the ^thesis group** t^^re oi^rgea with the responsibility of 
preparing qusstionnalres, oosfCllng data and preparing sundries# 3ach 
number was to give particular attention to one problem but the group as 
a whole would review queatioaaaires in their fiml form# 

Frm leaders in education, business, and industry the Survey 
Co.7a&ittea gathered opinions indicative of what the public thinks about 
education and of what possible solutions or remedies they believe should 
be applied to settle its probleiss# Previous studies wore consulted to 
find what work had been done in the field before and to avoid duplication. 
Magasine articles having any bearing on one or more phases of the Survey 
ware studied, and educational Journals were checked to find into what 
main groupings teachers* grievances might fall. Personal experience on 
oonssittees seeking to in^rove teachers* salaries and working conditions 
was drawn upon. 

With information frm these various sources, questionnaires were 
prepared and submitted to the Survey Committee to be printed and sent out#

yearns salary in Hamilton and Saskatoon if the teacher has the maximum 
aooumulatlon of SOO day*s sick leave credit* 

Fow centres provide any plan of cumulative sick leave wtth pay. 
la seven of the nine provinces reporting, the accumulation varies from 
20 to 200 days. Toronto and Montreal have no stated m^imm in their 
oomulative plans. Even the provincial statutes vary. Hrinoe Edward 
island makes no statutory provision for sick loave. Sew Brunswick allows 
2|' days per term, cumulative to a total of 20 days* British Coloiiibia 
grants one day’s sick pay for every month taught; unused sick leave in 
one year he treated aconsrulatively in the next up to a maximum of 
thirty teaching days. Other provinces usually have twenty days statutory 
sick leave and persiit ouaulative plans, but leave their details to the 
discretion of school boards, feeohers’ associations report very few. 
schemes la effect outside of British Oolumbia whom % great majority 
of boards” are reported to follow the plan outlined above and noted in 
the table for Westminster*

1. Salaries of Canadian Teaohers» August 1939 * 

Over 20^ of the teaohars in 1938 received less than ^S00« 

w 50^ « »» w « « rt « »♦ ;>300^ 

Over 10|000 teachers in 1938 were paid less than the minimxan wage for 
untrained girls in industr^t 1460» 

Over 19«000 (about one-third) were paid less than the minimum for 
trained girls and women, #602* 

The economic condition of women teachers is deplorable* Over 8,000 
of them earn less than §400 per ^ear. 

The eoonomio plight of men teachers in all but the favored urban centres 
is ^VL&t as scandalous, fortj^-four per cent of all men teachers in 
1936-37 could not afford hy ordinary? Canadian standards to support a 
wife. About 38^ Of them were earning less than unskilled laborers. 


2* l*roblems and Principles in the Sphedul i ng of Teachers* Salaries t 

The Sosiaittee has examined the available studies of teachers* salaries 
and salary scheduling! has selected whet seem to them to be the more 
important problems involved! and hBs drafted this statement of principles* 

Chapter Headings* 

1 Who Should litee the Salary? Schedule? 

The construction of teachers* salary schedules and the 
formulation of salary policies should be undertaken Jointly by classroom 
teachers, school administrators, sehoolboard members and Interested laymen. 

li The Preparation Schedule 

The baste salary olasslfication of teachers in public school 
systems should be determined by professional and academic pmlifieations, 
regardless of the grade level of the pupils taught. 

1X1 Equal Pay for Men sad t©sa»n 

Teachers of equivalent preparation, experience, and teaching 
load should receive equal pay regardless of sex. 

IV Eating as Belated to Salary Payments 

The use of so-called efficiency ratings as a basis for salary 
awards Is impractical and inadvisable in public education. 

V Evaluation of Training, Experience, and Travel 

The relationship of professional and academic qualifications 
to the location of the teachers on the salary scale should be clearly 
stated in the rules and regulations governing the operation of the soheiule 
and regular appraisal should be made of individual teacher qualifications*

VI Salary inoroments 

Provision should be made In a salar;y schedule for 
definitely announoed salary inoreases to be awarded regularly at

Teachers’ salaries should be based in part on the oost of 
maintaining an appropriate standard of living. (Formulas are given) 


3. ^n Evaluation of the Social and Cultural Life of Teachers in Bural 

Cosaaunltles in ilberta i 

Teachers in rural ilberta are discontented because of*oertain grimness 
and meagreness In the whole texture of their enviroamant* Those things 
in TOdern society which are challenging and stimulating, which add 
comfort, and which add a certain softness and beauty to life seem to be 
absent from the life of a rural teacher. Une may say that this is true 
for all rural people in this province, and the teacher is not experionoing 
the exceptional in the rural eoirasuiiity, but this is not quite true. 

Because most rural teachers are young, single women, they have not the 
challenge of oonQuering the soil and the adversities of bature to build 
a hCHse, nor can they experience the beauty of intimate family life. 

• , « because she is a stranger, and because her type of work does not 
fit into this cooperative mnittitet, the rural family), (the teacher) is 
more isolated than she is likely to be in a type of society which has 
more diversified occupations. 

Tim teacher, also, puts down no real roots in the coR»imity because she 
is usually dissatisfied with the cultural pattern. 

Teaohereges rest^la a problem* » * . they still further isolate the 
teacher* * • • However, since eighty per cent of th© teachers who lived 
in teaoherages prefer this way of living to boarding with a family in 
the coiuaunity, one must look to some method of ia^roving that form of 
living. Perhaps the solution lies in having more than one live together. 

There is a deadly saijssess about them all (teaoherages )• Ho attes^t is 
made to give « • « this residence soe^ of the aspects of a home. 

There Is a great need in the education of teachers to give them some 
insight into the value of planning for leisure time and how to us© that 
time for greater personal satisfaction. 

There is also need to give teachers going into open country schools a 
great understanding of rural sociology*
